</div>
			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Elijah</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"></div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the prophet. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Elijah_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Elijah (disambiguation)">Elijah (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<table class="infobox biography vcard" style="width:22em">
<tr>
<th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold"><span class="fn">Elijah (Elias)</span></th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/File:Elijah_Icon_Sinai_c1200.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Elijah Icon Sinai c1200.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Elijah_Icon_Sinai_c1200.jpg/220px-Elijah_Icon_Sinai_c1200.jpg" width="220" height="413" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Elijah_Icon_Sinai_c1200.jpg/330px-Elijah_Icon_Sinai_c1200.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Elijah_Icon_Sinai_c1200.jpg/440px-Elijah_Icon_Sinai_c1200.jpg 2x" data-file-width="816" data-file-height="1531" /></a>
<div>An icon of Elijah from <a href="/wiki/Saint_Catherine%27s_Monastery" title="Saint Catherine's Monastery">Saint Catherine's Monastery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mount_Sinai" title="Mount Sinai">Mount Sinai</a>.</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>Elijah</b> (<a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a>: <span class="nowrap"><span lang="he" xml:lang="he"><span class="script-hebrew" style="font-size: 115%; font-family: Alef, 'SBL BibLit', 'SBL Hebrew', 'David CLM', 'Frenk Ruehl CLM', 'Hadasim CLM', Shofar, 'Ezra SIL', 'Ezra SIL SR', Cardo, 'Noto Sans Hebrew', David, FreeSerif, 'Times New Roman', FreeSans, Arial;" dir="rtl">אֱלִיָּהוּ</span>‎</span>,</span> <i>Eliyahu</i>, meaning "My <a href="/wiki/El_(deity)" title="El (deity)">God</a> is <a href="/wiki/Yahweh" title="Yahweh">Yahu</a>/<a href="/wiki/Jah" title="Jah">Jah</a>"<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>) or <b>Elias</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/aɪ/: 'i' in 'tide'">aɪ</span><span title="/./: syllable break">.</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el" xml:lang="el">Ηλίας</span> <i>Elías</i>; <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Syriac</a>: <span lang="syr" dir="rtl" xml:lang="syr">ܐܸܠܝܼܵܐ</span>‎ <i>Elyāe</i>; <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: إلياس or إليا, <i>Ilyās</i> or <i>Ilyā</i>) was a <a href="/wiki/Prophet" title="Prophet">prophet</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Thaumaturgy" title="Thaumaturgy">miracle worker</a> who lived in the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Israel_(Samaria)" title="Kingdom of Israel (Samaria)">northern kingdom of Israel</a><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Ahab" title="Ahab">Ahab</a> (<a href="/wiki/9th_century_BC" title="9th century BC">9th century BC</a>), according to the <a href="/wiki/Books_of_Kings" title="Books of Kings">Books of Kings</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">Hebrew Bible</a>. In 1 Kings 18, Elijah defended the worship of the <a href="/wiki/Yahweh" title="Yahweh">Jewish God</a> over that of the Canaanite deity <a href="/wiki/Baal" title="Baal">Baal</a>. God also performed many miracles through Elijah, including <a href="/wiki/Resurrection" title="Resurrection">resurrection</a> (raising the dead), bringing fire down from the sky, and <a href="/wiki/Entering_Heaven_alive" title="Entering Heaven alive">entering Heaven alive</a> "by a whirlwind".<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> He is also portrayed as leading a school of prophets known as "the sons of the prophets".<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Following his ascension, <a href="/wiki/Elisha" title="Elisha">Elisha</a> his disciple and most devoted assistant took over his role as leader of this school. The <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Malachi" title="Book of Malachi">Book of Malachi</a> prophesies Elijah's return "before the coming of the great and terrible day of the LORD",<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> making him a harbinger of the <a href="/wiki/Messiah" title="Messiah">Messiah</a> and of the <a href="/wiki/Eschatology" title="Eschatology">eschaton</a> in various faiths that revere the Hebrew Bible. References to Elijah appear in <a href="/wiki/Sirach" title="Sirach">Ecclesiasticus</a>, the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Mishnah" title="Mishnah">Mishnah</a> and <a href="/wiki/Talmud" title="Talmud">Talmud</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Mormon" title="Book of Mormon">Book of Mormon</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Doctrine_and_Covenants" title="Doctrine and Covenants">Doctrine and Covenants</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%27%C3%AD_Faith" title="Bahá'í Faith">Bahá'í</a> writings.</p>
<p>In Judaism, Elijah's name is invoked at the weekly <a href="/wiki/Havdalah" title="Havdalah">Havdalah</a> ritual that marks the end of <a href="/wiki/Shabbat" title="Shabbat">Shabbat</a>, and Elijah is invoked in other Jewish customs, among them the <a href="/wiki/Passover_Seder" title="Passover Seder">Passover Seder</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Brit_milah" title="Brit milah">brit milah</a> (ritual circumcision). He appears in numerous stories and references in the <a href="/wiki/Haggadah" title="Haggadah">Haggadah</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rabbinic_literature" title="Rabbinic literature">rabbinic literature</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Babylonian_Talmud" class="mw-redirect" title="Babylonian Talmud">Babylonian Talmud</a>.</p>
<p>The Christian <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> describes how Elijah was thought, by some, to be the <a href="/wiki/Messiah" title="Messiah">Messiah</a>. <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a> makes it clear that <a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">John the Baptist</a> is "the Elijah" who was promised to come in <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Malachi+4%3A5-6&amp;version=NIV">Malachi 4:5</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Elijah appears with <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">Moses</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Transfiguration_of_Jesus" title="Transfiguration of Jesus">Transfiguration of Jesus</a>. Elijah is also a figure in various Christian folk traditions, at times identified with earlier pagan thunder or sky gods.</p>
<p>In Islam, Elijah appears in the Quran as a <a href="/wiki/Prophets_and_messengers_in_Islam" title="Prophets and messengers in Islam">prophet and messenger of God</a>, where his biblical narrative of preaching against the worshipers of <a href="/wiki/Baal" title="Baal">Baal</a> is recounted in a concise form.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Due to his importance to Muslims, Catholics and Orthodox Christians, Elijah has been venerated as the <a href="/wiki/Patron_saint" title="Patron saint">patron saint</a> of <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Bosnia and Herzegovina">Bosnia and Herzegovina</a> since 1752.</p>
<p></p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-43"><a href="#Elijah_in_arts_and_literature"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Elijah in arts and literature</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-44"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-45"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-46"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a>

</li>

</div>
<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biblical_accounts">Biblical accounts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Biblical accounts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kingdoms_of_Israel_and_Judah_map_830.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Kingdoms_of_Israel_and_Judah_map_830.svg/220px-Kingdoms_of_Israel_and_Judah_map_830.svg.png" width="220" height="262" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Kingdoms_of_Israel_and_Judah_map_830.svg/330px-Kingdoms_of_Israel_and_Judah_map_830.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Kingdoms_of_Israel_and_Judah_map_830.svg/440px-Kingdoms_of_Israel_and_Judah_map_830.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="859" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kingdoms_of_Israel_and_Judah_map_830.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map of <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> as it was in the 9th century <a href="/wiki/Before_Christ" class="mw-redirect" title="Before Christ">BC</a>. <b>Blue</b> is the Kingdom of Israel. <b>Golden yellow</b> is the Kingdom of Judah.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>According to the Bible, by the 9th century BC, the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Israel_(united_monarchy)" title="Kingdom of Israel (united monarchy)">Kingdom of Israel</a>, once united under <a href="/wiki/Solomon" title="Solomon">Solomon</a>, was divided into the northern <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Israel_(Samaria)" title="Kingdom of Israel (Samaria)">Kingdom of Israel</a> and southern <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Judah" title="Kingdom of Judah">Kingdom of Judah</a>, which retained the historical capital of Jerusalem along with its <a href="/wiki/Temple_in_Jerusalem" title="Temple in Jerusalem">Temple</a>. However, scholars today are divided as to whether the united Kingdom under Solomon ever existed.<sup id="cite_ref-BakerArnold2004_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BakerArnold2004-10">[10]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Omri" title="Omri">Omri</a>, King of Israel, continued policies dating from the reign of <a href="/wiki/Jeroboam" title="Jeroboam">Jeroboam</a>, contrary to religious law, that were intended to reorient religious focus away from Jerusalem: encouraging the building of local temple altars for sacrifices, appointing priests from outside the family of the <a href="/wiki/Levite" title="Levite">Levites</a>, and allowing or encouraging temples dedicated to <a href="/wiki/Baal" title="Baal">Baal</a>, an important deity in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Canaanite_religion" title="Ancient Canaanite religion">ancient Canaanite religion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Omri achieved domestic security with a marriage alliance between his son <a href="/wiki/Ahab" title="Ahab">Ahab</a> and princess <a href="/wiki/Jezebel" title="Jezebel">Jezebel</a>, a priestess of Baal and the daughter of the king of <a href="/wiki/Sidon" title="Sidon">Sidon</a> in <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> These solutions brought security and economic prosperity to Israel for a time,<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> but did not bring peace with the Israelite prophets, who were interested in a strict <a href="/wiki/Deuteronomic_Code" title="Deuteronomic Code">deuteronomic interpretation</a> of the religious law.</p>
<p>Under Ahab's kingship, these tensions were exacerbated. Ahab built a temple for Baal, and his wife Jezebel brought a large entourage of priests and prophets of Baal and <a href="/wiki/Asherah" title="Asherah">Asherah</a> into the country. It is in this context that Elijah is introduced in <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt09a17.htm#1">1 Kings 17:1</a> as Elijah "the <a href="/wiki/Tishbite" title="Tishbite">Tishbite</a>". He warns Ahab that there will be years of catastrophic <a href="/wiki/Drought" title="Drought">drought</a> so severe that not even <a href="/wiki/Dew" title="Dew">dew</a> will form, because Ahab and his queen stand at the end of a line of kings of Israel who are said to have "done evil in the sight of the Lord."</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1st_and_2nd_Kings">1st and 2nd Kings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: 1st and 2nd Kings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Washington_Allston_-_Elijah_in_the_Desert_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Washington_Allston_-_Elijah_in_the_Desert_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Washington_Allston_-_Elijah_in_the_Desert_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Washington_Allston_-_Elijah_in_the_Desert_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Washington_Allston_-_Elijah_in_the_Desert_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Washington_Allston_-_Elijah_in_the_Desert_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Washington_Allston_-_Elijah_in_the_Desert_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4945" data-file-height="3319" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Washington_Allston_-_Elijah_in_the_Desert_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Elijah in the wilderness</i>, by <a href="/wiki/Washington_Allston" title="Washington Allston">Washington Allston</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>No background for the person of Elijah is given except for his brief description as being a "Tishbite." His name in Hebrew means "My God is Yahweh", and may be a title applied to him because of his challenge to worship of Baal.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-jency_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jency-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>As told in the Hebrew Bible, Elijah's challenge is bold and direct. <a href="/wiki/Baal" title="Baal">Baal</a> was the Canaanite god responsible for rain, thunder, lightning, and dew. Elijah not only challenges Baal on behalf of God himself, he challenges Jezebel, her priests, Ahab and the people of Israel.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Widow_of_Zarephath">Widow of Zarephath</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Widow of Zarephath">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Raising_of_the_son_of_the_widow_of_Zarephath" title="Raising of the son of the widow of Zarephath">Raising of the son of the widow of Zarephath</a></div>
<p>After Elijah's confrontation with Ahab, God tells him to flee out of Israel, to a hiding place by the brook <a href="/wiki/Chorath" title="Chorath">Chorath</a>, east of the <a href="/wiki/Jordan_river" class="mw-redirect" title="Jordan river">Jordan</a>, where he will be fed by <a href="/wiki/Cultural_depictions_of_ravens" title="Cultural depictions of ravens">ravens</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> When the brook dries up, God sends him to a widow living in the town of <a href="/wiki/Sarepta" title="Sarepta">Zarephath</a> in <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicia</a>. When Elijah finds her and asks to be fed, she says that she does not have sufficient food to keep her and her own son alive. Elijah tells her that God will not allow her supply of flour or oil to run out, saying, “Do not be afraid ... For thus says the Lord the God of Israel: The jar of meal will not be emptied and the jug of oil will not fail until the day that the Lord sends rain on the earth.”<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> She feeds him the last of their food, and Elijah's promise miraculously comes true. God gave her "<a href="/wiki/Manna" title="Manna">manna</a>" from heaven even while he was withholding food from his unfaithful people in the promised land. Some time later the widow's son dies and the widow cries, "You have come to me to bring my sin to remembrance, and to cause the death of my son!"<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Elijah prays that God might restore her son so that the trustworthiness of God's word might be demonstrated. 1 Kings 17:22 relates how God "listened to the voice of Elijah; the life of the child came into him again, and he revived."<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> This is the first instance of raising the dead recorded in Scripture. This non-Israelite widow was granted the life of her son, the only hope for a widow in ancient society. The widow cried, "...the word of the Lord in your mouth is truth."<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> She made a confession that the Israelites had failed to make.</p>
<p>After more than three years of drought and famine, God tells Elijah to return to Ahab and announce the end of the drought: not occasioned by repentance in Israel but by the command of the Lord, who had determined to reveal himself again to his people. While on his way, Elijah meets <a href="/wiki/Obadiah_(1_Kings)" title="Obadiah (1 Kings)">Obadiah</a>, the head of Ahab's household, who had hidden a hundred Jewish prophets when Ahab and Jezebel had been killing them. Elijah sends Obadiah back to Ahab to announce his return to Israel.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Challenge_to_Baal">Challenge to Baal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Challenge to Baal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:JPF-Cave_Of_Elijah.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/JPF-Cave_Of_Elijah.JPG/220px-JPF-Cave_Of_Elijah.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/JPF-Cave_Of_Elijah.JPG/330px-JPF-Cave_Of_Elijah.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/JPF-Cave_Of_Elijah.JPG/440px-JPF-Cave_Of_Elijah.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="900" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:JPF-Cave_Of_Elijah.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A statue of Elijah in the Cave of Elijah, <a href="/wiki/Mount_Carmel" title="Mount Carmel">Mount Carmel</a>, Israel.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Elijahwindow.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Elijahwindow.jpg/220px-Elijahwindow.jpg" width="220" height="488" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Elijahwindow.jpg/330px-Elijahwindow.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Elijahwindow.jpg/440px-Elijahwindow.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1925" data-file-height="4270" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Elijahwindow.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Elijah's offering is consumed by fire from heaven in a stained glass window at <a href="/wiki/St._Matthew%27s_German_Evangelical_Lutheran_Church" title="St. Matthew's German Evangelical Lutheran Church">St. Matthew's German Evangelical Lutheran Church</a> in Charleston, South Carolina</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When Ahab confronts Elijah, he refers to him as the "troubler of Israel." Elijah responds by throwing the charge back at Ahab, saying that it is Ahab who has troubled Israel by allowing the worship of <a href="/wiki/False_god" title="False god">false gods</a>. Elijah then berates both the people of Israel and Ahab for their acquiescence in Baal worship. "How long will you go limping with two different opinions? If the Lord is God, follow him; but if Baal, then follow him."<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> And the people were silent. The Hebrew for this word, "go limping" or "waver", is the same as that used for "danced" in verse 26, where the prophets of Baal frantically dance. Elijah speaks with sharp irony: in the religious ambivalence of Israel, he is portraying here as engaging in a wild and futile religious "dance".</p>
<p>At this point Elijah proposes a direct test of the powers of Baal and the Jewish God. The people of Israel, 450 prophets of Baal, and 400 prophets of <a href="/wiki/Asherah" title="Asherah">Asherah</a> are summoned to <a href="/wiki/Mount_Carmel" title="Mount Carmel">Mount Carmel</a>. Two altars are built, one for Baal and one for God. Wood is laid on the altars. Two oxen are slaughtered and cut into pieces; the pieces are laid on the wood. Elijah then invites the priests of Baal to pray for fire to light the sacrifice. They pray from morning to noon without success. Elijah ridicules their efforts. "At noon Elijah mocked them, saying, ‘Cry aloud! Surely he is a god; either he is meditating, or he has wandered away, or he is on a journey, or perhaps he is asleep and must be awakened.’"<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> They respond by cutting themselves and adding their own blood to the sacrifice (such mutilation of the body was strictly forbidden in the Mosaic law). They continue praying until evening without success.</p>
<p>Elijah now orders that the altar of his god be drenched with water from "four large jars" poured three times.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> He asks God to accept the sacrifice. Fire falls from the sky, consuming the water, the sacrifice and the stones of the altar itself as well. Elijah then orders the deaths of the prophets of Baal. Elijah prays earnestly for rain to fall again on the land. Then the rains begin, signaling the end of the famine.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mount_Horeb">Mount Horeb</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Mount Horeb">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Jezebel_(Bible)" class="mw-redirect" title="Jezebel (Bible)">Jezebel</a>, enraged that Elijah had ordered the deaths of her priests, threatens to kill Elijah.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Later Elijah would prophesy about Jezebel's death, because of her sin. Later, Elijah flees to <a href="/wiki/Beersheba" title="Beersheba">Beersheba</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Judah" title="Kingdom of Judah">Judah</a>, continues alone into the wilderness, and finally sits down under a shrub, praying for death. He falls asleep under the tree; the <a href="/wiki/Angel_of_the_Lord" title="Angel of the Lord">angel of the Lord</a> touches him and tells him to wake up and eat. When he awakens he finds bread and a jar of water. He eats, drinks, and goes back to sleep. The angel comes a second time and tells him to eat and drink because he has a long journey ahead of him.</p>
<p>Elijah travels for forty days and forty nights to <a href="/wiki/Mount_Horeb" title="Mount Horeb">Mount Horeb</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">Moses</a> had received the <a href="/wiki/Ten_Commandments" title="Ten Commandments">Ten Commandments</a>. Elijah is the only person described in the Bible as returning to Horeb, after Moses and his generation had left Horeb several centuries before. He seeks shelter in a <a href="/wiki/Cave_of_Elijah" title="Cave of Elijah">cave</a>. God again speaks to Elijah:<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> "What doest thou here, Elijah?". Elijah did not give a direct answer to the Lord's question but evades and equivocates, implying that the work the Lord had begun centuries earlier had now come to nothing, and that his own work was fruitless. Unlike Moses, who tried to defend Israel when they sinned with the golden calf, Elijah bitterly complains over the Israelites' unfaithfulness and says he is the "only one left". Up until this time Elijah has only the word of God to guide him, but now he is told to go outside the cave and "stand before the Lord." A terrible wind passes, but God is not in the wind. A great earthquake shakes the mountain, but God is not in the earthquake. Then a fire passes the mountain, but God is not in the fire. Then a "still small voice" comes to Elijah and asks again, "What doest thou here, Elijah?" Elijah again evades the question and his lament is unrevised, showing that he did not understand the importance of the divine revelation he had just witnessed. God then sends him out again, this time to <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a> to anoint <a href="/wiki/Hazael" title="Hazael">Hazael</a> as king of <a href="/wiki/Aram-Damascus" title="Aram-Damascus">Aram</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jehu" title="Jehu">Jehu</a> as king of <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Elisha" title="Elisha">Elisha</a> as his replacement.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Elijah%27s_cave_P3160005.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Elijah%27s_cave_P3160005.JPG/220px-Elijah%27s_cave_P3160005.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Elijah%27s_cave_P3160005.JPG/330px-Elijah%27s_cave_P3160005.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Elijah%27s_cave_P3160005.JPG/440px-Elijah%27s_cave_P3160005.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2288" data-file-height="1712" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Elijah%27s_cave_P3160005.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Cave of Elijah, <a href="/wiki/Mount_Carmel" title="Mount Carmel">Mount Carmel</a>, Israel.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Vineyard_of_Naboth">Vineyard of Naboth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Vineyard of Naboth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Elijah encounters Ahab again in <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt09a21.htm#1">1 Kings 21</a>, after Ahab has acquired possession of a vineyard by murder. Ahab desires to have the vineyard of <a href="/wiki/Naboth" title="Naboth">Naboth</a> of <a href="/wiki/Jezreel_(city)" title="Jezreel (city)">Jezreel</a>. He offers a better vineyard or a fair price for the land. But Naboth tells Ahab that God has told him not to part with the land. Ahab accepts this answer with sullen bad grace. Jezebel, however, plots a method for acquiring the land. She sends letters, in Ahab's name, to the elders and nobles who lived near Naboth. They are to arrange a feast and invite Naboth. At the feast, false charges of cursing God and Ahab are to be made against him. The plot is carried out and Naboth is stoned to death. When word comes that Naboth is dead, Jezebel tells Ahab to take possession of the vineyard.</p>
<p>God again speaks to Elijah and sends him to confront Ahab with a question and a prophecy: "Have you killed, and also taken possession?" and, "In the place where dogs licked up the blood of Naboth, dogs will also lick up your blood."<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Ahab begins the confrontation by calling Elijah his enemy. Elijah responds by throwing the charge back at him, telling him that he has made himself the enemy of God by his own actions. Elijah then goes beyond the prophecy he was given and tells Ahab that his entire kingdom will reject his authority; that Jezebel will be eaten by dogs within Jezreel; and that his family will be consumed by dogs as well (if they die in a city) or by birds (if they die in the country). When Ahab hears this he repents to such a degree that God relents in punishing Ahab but will punish Jezebel and their son: <a href="/wiki/Ahaziah_of_Israel" title="Ahaziah of Israel">Ahaziah</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ahaziah">Ahaziah</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Ahaziah">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Elijah.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Elijah.jpg/220px-Elijah.jpg" width="220" height="267" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Elijah.jpg/330px-Elijah.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c6/Elijah.jpg 2x" data-file-width="412" data-file-height="500" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Elijah.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Russian_icon" class="mw-redirect" title="Russian icon">Russian icon</a> of the Prophet Elijah, 18th century (<a href="/wiki/Iconostasis" title="Iconostasis">Iconostasis</a> of <a href="/wiki/Kizhi" class="mw-redirect" title="Kizhi">Kizhi</a> <a href="/wiki/Monastery" title="Monastery">monastery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karelia" title="Karelia">Karelia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Elijah continues now from Ahab to an encounter with <a href="/wiki/Ahaziah_of_Israel" title="Ahaziah of Israel">Ahaziah</a>. The scene opens with Ahaziah seriously injured in a fall. He sends to the priests of <a href="/wiki/Beelzebub" title="Beelzebub">Baalzebub</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ekron" title="Ekron">Ekron</a>, outside the kingdom of Israel, to know if he will recover. Elijah intercepts his messengers and sends them back to Ahaziah with a message "Is it because there is no God in Israel that you are sending to inquire of Baal-zebub, the god of Ekron?"<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Ahaziah asks the messengers to describe the person who gave them this message. They tell him he was a hairy man with a leather belt around his waist and he instantly recognizes the description as Elijah the Tishbite.</p>
<p>Ahaziah sends out three groups of soldiers to arrest Elijah. The first two are destroyed by fire which Elijah calls down from heaven. The leader of the third group asks for mercy for himself and his men. Elijah agrees to accompany this third group to Ahaziah, where he gives his prophecy in person.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Departure">Departure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Departure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Elijah_Departure_and_Elisha.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Elijah_Departure_and_Elisha.jpg/150px-Elijah_Departure_and_Elisha.jpg" width="150" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Elijah_Departure_and_Elisha.jpg/225px-Elijah_Departure_and_Elisha.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Elijah_Departure_and_Elisha.jpg/300px-Elijah_Departure_and_Elisha.jpg 2x" data-file-width="337" data-file-height="450" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Elijah_Departure_and_Elisha.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Elijah Departure and Elisha, Russian Orthodox icon of 17th century</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>According to <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt09b02.htm#3">2 Kings 2:3–9</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elisha" title="Elisha">Elisha</a> (Eliseus) and "the sons of the prophets" knew beforehand that Elijah would one day be assumed into heaven. Elisha asked Elijah to "let a double portion" of Elijah's "spirit" be upon him. Elijah agreed, with the condition that Elisha would see him be "taken".</p>
<p>Elijah, in company with Elisha, approaches the Jordan. He rolls up his <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(vesture)" title="Mantle (vesture)">mantle</a> and strikes the water.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> The water immediately divides and Elijah and Elisha cross on dry land. Suddenly, a chariot of fire and horses of fire appear and Elijah is lifted up in a whirlwind. As Elijah is lifted up, his mantle falls to the ground and Elisha picks it up.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Final_mention:_2nd_Chronicles">Final mention: 2nd Chronicles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Final mention: 2nd Chronicles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:SaintIliaFromRilaMonastery.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/SaintIliaFromRilaMonastery.jpg/220px-SaintIliaFromRilaMonastery.jpg" width="220" height="133" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/SaintIliaFromRilaMonastery.jpg/330px-SaintIliaFromRilaMonastery.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/SaintIliaFromRilaMonastery.jpg/440px-SaintIliaFromRilaMonastery.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2074" data-file-height="1252" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:SaintIliaFromRilaMonastery.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Saint Elias in the cave (below) and on a chariot of fire. A fresco from <a href="/wiki/Rila_Monastery" title="Rila Monastery">Rila Monastery</a>, Bulgaria, medieval <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Orthodox</a> tradition, renovated 20th century</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Elijah is mentioned once more in <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt25b21.htm#12">2 Chronicles 21:12</a>, which will be his final mention in the Hebrew Bible. A letter is sent under the prophet's name to <a href="/wiki/Jehoram_of_Judah" title="Jehoram of Judah">Jehoram of Judah</a>. It tells him that he has led the people of Judah astray in the same way that Israel was led astray. The prophet ends the letter with a prediction of a painful death. This letter is a puzzle to readers for several reasons. First, it concerns a king of the southern kingdom, while Elijah concerned himself with the kingdom of Israel. Second, the message begins with "Thus says <a href="/wiki/YHVH" class="mw-redirect" title="YHVH">YHVH</a>, God of your father David..." rather than the more usual "...in the name of <a href="/wiki/YHVH" class="mw-redirect" title="YHVH">YHVH</a> the God of Israel." Also, this letter seems to come after Elijah's ascension into the whirlwind. <a href="/w/index.php?title=Michael_Wilcock&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Michael Wilcock (page does not exist)">Michael Wilcock</a>, formerly of <a href="/wiki/Trinity_College,_Bristol" title="Trinity College, Bristol">Trinity College, Bristol</a>, suggests a number of possible reasons for this letter, among them that it may be an example of a better known prophet's name being substituted for that of a lesser known prophet.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Van_Seters" title="John Van Seters">John Van Seters</a>, however, rejects the letter as having any connection with the Elijah tradition.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> However, Wilcock argues that Elijah's letter, 'does address a very 'northern' situation in the southern kingdom', and thus is authentic.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Christian_end_of_Elijah_in_Malachi">The Christian end of Elijah in Malachi</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: The Christian end of Elijah in Malachi">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="toccolours" style="float: right; margin-left: 1em; margin-right: 2em; font-size: 85%; background:#c6dbf7; color:black; width:30em; max-width: 40%;" cellspacing="5">
<tr>
<td style="text-align: left;">
<p>"Lo, I will send you the prophet Elijah before the great and terrible day of the Lord comes. He will turn the hearts of parents to their children and the hearts of children to their parents, so that I will not come and strike the land with a curse."</p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align: left;">— Malachi 4:5–6, <i>New Revised Standard Version</i></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>While the final mention of Elijah in the Hebrew Bible is in the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Chronicles" class="mw-redirect" title="Book of Chronicles">Book of Chronicles</a>, the Christian Bible's ordering of the books of the <a href="/wiki/Septuagint" title="Septuagint">Septuagint</a> places the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Malachi" title="Book of Malachi">Book of Malachi</a>, which prophesies a messiah, before the Gospels <sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> and means that Elijah's final Old Testament appearance is in the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Malachi" title="Book of Malachi">Book of Malachi</a>, where it is written, "Lo, I will send you the prophet Elijah before the great and terrible day of the Lord comes."<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> That day is described as the burning of a great furnace, "... so that it will leave them neither root nor branch."<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> In Christianity it is traditionally believed that Elijah's appearance during the <a href="/wiki/Transfiguration_of_Jesus" title="Transfiguration of Jesus">transfiguration of Jesus</a> fulfilled this prophecy. Moreover, in the Gospel of Matthew, Jesus identifies John the Baptist as the spiritual successor to Elijah: "and if you are willing to accept it, he is Elijah who is to come."<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Finally, the verses in Malachi are believed to indicate that Elijah has a role in the end-times, immediately before the second coming of Jesus.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="One_theory_of_textual_analysis">One theory of textual analysis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: One theory of textual analysis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to Susanne Otto, the Elijah stories were added to the <a href="/wiki/Deuteronomistic_History" class="mw-redirect" title="Deuteronomistic History">Deuteronomistic History</a> in four stages. The first stage dates from the final edition of the History, about 560 BC, when the three stories of <a href="/wiki/Naboth" title="Naboth">Naboth</a>’s vineyard, the death of <a href="/wiki/Ahaziah_of_Israel" title="Ahaziah of Israel">Ahaziah</a>, and the story of <a href="/wiki/Jehu" title="Jehu">Jehu</a>’s coup were included to embody the themes of the reliability of God's word and the cycle of Baal worship and religious reform in the history of the Northern Kingdom. The narratives about the <a href="/wiki/Omri" title="Omri">Omride</a> wars were added shortly afterwards to illustrate a newly introduced theme, that the attitude of the king towards God determines the fate of Israel. According to Otto, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt09a17.htm#1">1 Kings 17–18</a> was added in early post-Exilic times (after 538 BC) to demonstrate the possibility of a new life in community with God after the time of judgment. Additionally, Otto suggests that in the fifth century BC, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt09a19.htm#1">1 Kings 19:1–18</a> and the remaining <a href="/wiki/Elisha" title="Elisha">Elisha</a> stories were inserted to give prophecy a legitimate foundation in the history of Israel. The foregoing Otto analysis is heavily disputed amongst biblical scholars.<sup id="cite_ref-otto_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-otto-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_Aggadah_and_Talmud">In the Aggadah and Talmud</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: In the Aggadah and Talmud">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Jewish legends about Elijah abound in the <a href="/wiki/Aggadah" title="Aggadah">aggadah</a>, which is found throughout various collections of <a href="/wiki/Rabbinic_literature" title="Rabbinic literature">rabbinic literature</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Babylonian_Talmud" class="mw-redirect" title="Babylonian Talmud">Babylonian Talmud</a>. This varied literature does not merely discuss his life, but has created a new history of him, which, beginning with his death&#160;–  or "translation"&#160;–  ends only with the close of the history of the human race. The volume of references to Elijah in Jewish Tradition stands in marked contrast to that in the Canon. As in the case of most figures of Jewish legend, so in the case of Elijah, the biblical account became the basis of later legend. Elijah the precursor of the Messiah, Elijah zealous in the cause of God, Elijah the helper in distress: these are the three leading notes struck by the Aggadah, endeavoring to complete the biblical picture with the Elijah legends. His career is extensive, colorful, and varied. He has appeared the world over in the guise of a beggar and scholar.</p>
<p>From the time of <a href="/wiki/Malachi" title="Malachi">Malachi</a>, who says of Elijah that God will send him before "the great and dreadful day" (Mal. 3:23), down to the later stories of the Chasidic rabbis, reverence and love, expectation and hope, were always connected in the Jewish consciousness with Elijah.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin">Origin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Origin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Three different theories regarding Elijah's origin are presented in the Aggadah literature: (1) he belonged to the tribe of Gad,<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> (2) he was a Benjamite from Jerusalem, identical with the Elijah mentioned in I Chronicles 8:27, and (3) he was a priest.</p>
<p>Many Christian Church fathers also<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> have stated that Elijah was a priest. Some Rabbis have speculated that he should be identified with <a href="/wiki/Phinehas" title="Phinehas">Phinehas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to later <a href="/wiki/Kabbalah" title="Kabbalah">Kabbalistic</a> literature,<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Elijah was really an <a href="/wiki/Angel" title="Angel">angel</a> in human form, so that he had neither parents nor offspring.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> See <a href="/wiki/Melchizedek" title="Melchizedek">Melchizedek</a>.</p>
<p>The Midrash Rabbah Exodus 4:2 states "Elijah should have revived his parents as he had revived the son of the Zarephathite" indicating he surely had parents.</p>
<p>The Talmud states "Said he [Rabbah] to him (Elijah): Art thou not a priest: why then dost thou stand in a cemetery?"<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Elijah's_zeal_for_God"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Elijah.27s_zeal_for_God">Elijah's zeal for God</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Elijah's zeal for God">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Statue_of_Saint_Elijah_at_Saint_Elijah_Maronite_Cathedral,_Aleppo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Statue_of_Saint_Elijah_at_Saint_Elijah_Maronite_Cathedral%2C_Aleppo.jpg/170px-Statue_of_Saint_Elijah_at_Saint_Elijah_Maronite_Cathedral%2C_Aleppo.jpg" width="170" height="263" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Statue_of_Saint_Elijah_at_Saint_Elijah_Maronite_Cathedral%2C_Aleppo.jpg/255px-Statue_of_Saint_Elijah_at_Saint_Elijah_Maronite_Cathedral%2C_Aleppo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Statue_of_Saint_Elijah_at_Saint_Elijah_Maronite_Cathedral%2C_Aleppo.jpg/340px-Statue_of_Saint_Elijah_at_Saint_Elijah_Maronite_Cathedral%2C_Aleppo.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1980" data-file-height="3065" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Statue_of_Saint_Elijah_at_Saint_Elijah_Maronite_Cathedral,_Aleppo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The statue of Elijah at the <a href="/wiki/Saint_Elias_Cathedral" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Elias Cathedral">Saint Elias Cathedral</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aleppo" title="Aleppo">Aleppo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In spite of Elijah's many miracles, the mass of the Jewish people remained as godless as before. A <a href="/wiki/Midrash" title="Midrash">midrash</a> tells that they even abolished the sign of the covenant, and the prophet had to appear as Israel's accuser before God.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the same cave where God once appeared to Moses and revealed Himself as gracious and merciful, Elijah was summoned to appear before God. By this summons he perceived that he should have appealed to God's mercy, instead of becoming Israel's accuser. The prophet, however, remained relentless in his zeal and severity, so that God commanded him to appoint his successor.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>The vision in which God revealed Himself to Elijah gave him at the same time a picture of the destinies of man, who has to pass through "four worlds." This world was shown to the prophet by God through symbolism: in the form of the wind, since it disappears as the wind; storm is the day of death, before which man trembles; fire is the judgment in Gehenna; and the stillness is the last day.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>Three years after this vision<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Elijah was "translated." Concerning the place to which Elijah was transferred, opinions differ among Jews and Christians, but the old view was that Elijah was received among the heavenly inhabitants, where he records the deeds of men,<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> a task which according to the apocalyptic literature is entrusted to Enoch.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>But as early as the middle of the 2nd century, when the notion of translation to heaven underwent divergent possible interpretations by Christian theologians, the assertion was made that Elijah never entered into heaven proper.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> In later literature paradise is generally designated as the abode of Elijah,<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> but since the location of paradise is itself uncertain, the last two statements may be identical.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Elijah_in_Judaism">Elijah in Judaism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Elijah in Judaism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="Elijah's_chair"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Elijah.27s_chair">Elijah's chair</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Elijah's chair">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Brit_milah" title="Brit milah">Brit milah</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chair_of_Elijah.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Chair_of_Elijah.JPG/220px-Chair_of_Elijah.JPG" width="220" height="202" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Chair_of_Elijah.JPG/330px-Chair_of_Elijah.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2e/Chair_of_Elijah.JPG 2x" data-file-width="366" data-file-height="336" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chair_of_Elijah.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
"Chair of Elijah" used during the <a href="/wiki/Brit_milah" title="Brit milah">brit milah</a> (circumcision) ceremony. The <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_(language)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hebrew (language)">Hebrew</a> inscription reads "This is the chair of Elijah, remembered for Good."</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At Jewish <a href="/wiki/Brit_milah" title="Brit milah">circumcision</a> ceremonies, a chair is set aside for the use of the prophet Elijah. Elijah is said to be a <a href="/wiki/Witness" title="Witness">witness</a> at all circumcisions when the sign of the <a href="/wiki/Covenant_(religion)" title="Covenant (religion)">covenant</a> is placed upon the body of the child. This custom stems from the incident at <a href="/wiki/Mount_Horeb" title="Mount Horeb">Mount Horeb</a> (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt09a19.htm#1">1 Kings 19</a>): Elijah had arrived at Mount Horeb after the demonstration of God's presence and power on <a href="/wiki/Mount_Carmel" title="Mount Carmel">Mount Carmel</a>. (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt09a18.htm#1">1 Kings 18</a>) God asks Elijah to explain his arrival, and Elijah replies: "I have been very jealous for the Lord, the God of hosts; for the people of Israel have forsaken thy covenant, thrown down thy altars, and slain thy prophets with the sword; and I, even I only, am left; and they seek my life, to take it away" (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt09a19.htm#10">1 Kings 19:10</a>). According to Rabbinic tradition, Elijah's words were patently untrue (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt09a18.htm#4">1 Kings 18:4</a> and <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt09a19.htm#18">1 Kings 19:18</a>), and since Elijah accused Israel of failing to uphold the covenant, God would require Elijah to be present at every covenant of circumcision.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Elijah's_cup"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Elijah.27s_cup">Elijah's cup</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Elijah's cup">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Passover_Seder" title="Passover Seder">Passover Seder</a></div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Talmud" title="Talmud">Talmudic literature</a>, Elijah would visit <a href="/wiki/Rabbis" class="mw-redirect" title="Rabbis">rabbis</a> to help solve particularly difficult legal problems. Malachi had cited Elijah as the harbinger of the <a href="/wiki/Jewish_Eschatology" class="mw-redirect" title="Jewish Eschatology">eschaton</a>. Thus, when confronted with reconciling impossibly conflicting laws or rituals, the rabbis would set aside any decision "until Elijah comes."<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>One such decision was whether the <a href="/wiki/Passover_Seder" title="Passover Seder">Passover Seder</a> required four or five cups of wine. Each serving of wine corresponds to one of the "four expressions of redemption" in the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Exodus" title="Book of Exodus">Book of Exodus</a>:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>"I am the Lord, and <i>I will bring you out</i> from under the burdens of the Egyptians, and <i>I will deliver you</i> from their bondage, and <i>I will redeem you</i> with an out-stretched arm and with great acts of judgment, and <i>I will take you</i> for my people, and I will be your God; and you shall know that I am the Lord your God, who has brought you out from under the burdens of the Egyptians" (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0206.htm#6">Exodus 6:6–7</a>).</p>
</blockquote>
<p>The next verse, "And <i>I will bring you</i> into the land which I swore to give to Abraham, to Isaac, and to Jacob; I will give it to you for a possession. I am the Lord." (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0206.htm#8">Exodus 6:8</a>) was not fulfilled until the generation following the Passover story, and the rabbis could not decide whether this verse counted as part of the Passover celebration (thus deserving of another serving of wine). Thus, a cup was left for the arrival of Elijah.</p>
<p>In practice the fifth cup has come to be seen as a celebration of future redemption. Today, a place is reserved at the seder table and a cup of wine is placed there for Elijah. During the seder, the door of the house is opened and Elijah is invited in. Traditionally, the cup is viewed as Elijah’s and is used for no other purpose.<sup id="cite_ref-Temple_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Temple-56">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Havdalah">Havdalah</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Havdalah">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Havdalah" title="Havdalah">Havdalah</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Havdalah" title="Havdalah">Havdalah</a> is the ceremony that concludes the <a href="/wiki/Shabbat" title="Shabbat">Sabbath Day</a> (Saturday evening in Jewish tradition). As part of the concluding <a href="/wiki/Hymn" title="Hymn">hymn</a>, an appeal is made to God that Elijah will come during the following week. "Elijah the Prophet, Elijah the Tishbite. Let him come quickly, in our day with the messiah, the son of David."<sup id="cite_ref-Temple_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Temple-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Elijah_in_Jewish_folklore">Elijah in Jewish folklore</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Elijah in Jewish folklore">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The volume of references to Elijah in folklore stands in marked contrast to that in the canon. Elijah's miraculous transferral to heaven led to speculation as to his true identity. Louis Ginzberg equates him with Phinehas the grandson of Aaron<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0206.htm#25">Exodus 6:25</a>). Because of Phinehas' zealousness for God, he and his descendants were promised, "a covenant of lasting priesthood" (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0425.htm#13">Numbers 25:13</a>). Therefore, Elijah is a priest as well as a prophet. Elijah is also equated with the Archangel <a href="/wiki/Sandalphon" title="Sandalphon">Sandalphon</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> whose four wing beats will carry him to any part of the earth. When forced to choose between death and dishonor, Rabbi Kahana chose to leap to his death. Before he could strike the ground, Elijah/Sandalphon had appeared to catch him.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Yet another name for Elijah is "Angel of the Covenant"<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rabbi_Joshua_ben_Levi">Rabbi Joshua ben Levi</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Rabbi Joshua ben Levi">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>References to Elijah in Jewish folklore range from short observations (e. g. It is said that when dogs are happy for no reason, it is because Elijah is in the neighborhood)<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> to lengthy parables on the nature of God’s justice.</p>
<p>One such story is that of Rabbi <a href="/wiki/Joshua_ben_Levi" title="Joshua ben Levi">Joshua ben Levi</a>. The rabbi, a friend of Elijah’s, was asked what favor he might wish. The rabbi answered only that he be able to join Elijah in his wanderings. Elijah granted his wish only if he refrained from asking any questions about any of the prophet’s actions. He agreed and they began their journey. The first place they came to was the house of an elderly couple who were so poor they had only one old cow. The old couple gave of their hospitality as best they could. The next morning, as the travelers left, Elijah prayed that the old cow would die and it did. The second place they came to was the home of a wealthy man. He had no patience for his visitors and chased them away with the admonition that they should get jobs and not beg from honest people. As they were leaving, they passed the man’s wall and saw that it was crumbling. Elijah prayed that the wall be repaired and it was so. Next, they came to a wealthy synagogue. They were allowed to spend the night with only the smallest of provisions. When they left, Elijah prayed that every member of the synagogue might become a leader.</p>
<p>Finally, they came to a very poor synagogue. Here they were treated with great courtesy and hospitality. When they left, Elijah prayed that God might give them a single wise leader. At this Rabbi Joshua could no longer hold back. He demanded of Elijah an explanation of his actions. At the house of the old couple, Elijah knew that the Angel of Death was coming for the old woman. So he prayed that God might have the angel take the cow instead. At the house of the wealthy man, there was a great treasure hidden in the crumbling wall. Elijah prayed that the wall be restored thus keeping the treasure away from the miser. The story ends with a moral: A synagogue with many leaders will be ruined by many arguments. A town with a single wise leader will be guided to success and prosperity. "Know then, that if thou seest an evil-doer prosper, it is not always unto his advantage, and if a righteous man suffers need and distress, think not God is unjust."<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rabbi_Eliezer">Rabbi Eliezer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Rabbi Eliezer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Elijah of legend did not lose any of his ability to afflict the comfortable. The case of Rabbi Eliezer son of Rabbi <a href="/wiki/Shimon_bar_Yochai" class="mw-redirect" title="Shimon bar Yochai">Simon ben Yohai</a> is illustrative. Once, when walking a beach, he came upon a hideously ugly man—the prophet in disguise. The man greeted him courteously, "Peace be with thee, Rabbi." Instead of returning the greeting, the rabbi could not resist an insult, "How ugly you are! Is there anyone as ugly as you in your town?" Elijah responded with, "I don’t know. Perhaps you should tell the Master Architect how ugly is this, His construction." The rabbi realized his wrong and asked for pardon. But Elijah would not give it until the entire city had asked for forgiveness for the rabbi and the rabbi had promised to mend his ways.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lilith">Lilith</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Lilith">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Elijah was always seen as deeply pious, it seems only natural that he would be pitted against an equally evil individual. This was found in the person of <a href="/wiki/Lilith" title="Lilith">Lilith</a>. Lilith in legend was the first wife of Adam. She rebelled against Adam, the angels, and even God. She came to be seen as a demon and a witch.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>Elijah encountered Lilith and instantly recognized and challenged her, "Unclean one, where are you going?" Unable to avoid or lie to the prophet, she admitted she was on her way to the house of a pregnant woman. Her intention was to kill the woman and eat the child.</p>
<p>Elijah pronounced his malediction, "I curse you in the Name of the Lord. Be silent as a stone!" But, Lilith was able to make a bargain with Elijah. She promises to "forsake my evil ways" if Elijah will remove his curse. To seal the bargain she gives Elijah her names so that they can be posted in the houses of pregnant women or new born children or used as amulets. Lilith promises, "where I see those names, I shall run away at once. Neither the child nor the mother will ever be injured by me."<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Elijah_in_Christianity">Elijah in Christianity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Elijah in Christianity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="References_in_the_New_Testament">References in the New Testament</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: References in the New Testament">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Intesa_elijah.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Intesa_elijah.jpg/220px-Intesa_elijah.jpg" width="220" height="266" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Intesa_elijah.jpg/330px-Intesa_elijah.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Intesa_elijah.jpg/440px-Intesa_elijah.jpg 2x" data-file-width="517" data-file-height="626" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Intesa_elijah.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A Northern Russian icon from ca. 1290 showing the ascent of Elijah toward heaven</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a> would say for those who believed, <a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">John the Baptist</a> was Elijah, who would come before the "great and terrible day" as predicted by <a href="/wiki/Malachi" title="Malachi">Malachi</a>.</p>
<p>Some English translations of the New Testament use <a href="/wiki/Elias" title="Elias">Elias</a>, a Latin form of the name. In the <a href="/wiki/King_James_Version" title="King James Version">King James Version</a>, "Elias" appears only in the texts translated from Greek.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="John_the_Baptist">John the Baptist</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: John the Baptist">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">John the Baptist</a> preached a message of repentance and baptism. He predicted the day of judgment using imagery similar to that of Malachi. He also preached that the Messiah was coming. All of this was done in a style that immediately recalled the image of Elijah to his audience. He wore a coat of camel's hair secured with a leather girdle (<a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Matthew#3:4" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/Matthew">Matthew 3:4</a>, <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Mark#1:6" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/Mark">Mark 1:6</a>). He also frequently preached in wilderness areas near the Jordan River.</p>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_John" title="Gospel of John">Gospel of John</a>, when John the Baptist was asked by a delegation of priests (present tense) "Art thou Elias", he replied "I am not" (<a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/John#1:21" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/John">John 1:21</a>). <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Matthew#11:14" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/Matthew">Matthew 11:14</a> and <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Matthew#17:10" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/Matthew">Matthew 17:10–13</a> however, make it clear that John was the spiritual successor to Elijah. In the <a href="/wiki/Nativity_of_St._John_the_Baptist" class="mw-redirect" title="Nativity of St. John the Baptist">Nativity of St. John the Baptist</a> in Luke, <a href="/wiki/Gabriel" title="Gabriel">Gabriel</a> appears to <a href="/wiki/Zechariah_(priest)" title="Zechariah (priest)">Zechariah</a>, John's father, and told him that John "will turn many of the sons of Israel to the Lord their God," and that he will go forth "in the spirit and power of Elijah" (<a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Luke#1:16" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/Luke">Luke 1:16–17</a>).</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Jesus_Christ">Jesus Christ</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Jesus Christ">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Transfigurationbloch.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Transfigurationbloch.jpg/220px-Transfigurationbloch.jpg" width="220" height="273" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Transfigurationbloch.jpg/330px-Transfigurationbloch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Transfigurationbloch.jpg/440px-Transfigurationbloch.jpg 2x" data-file-width="734" data-file-height="912" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Transfigurationbloch.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Elijah appeared at the <a href="/wiki/Transfiguration_of_Jesus" title="Transfiguration of Jesus">Transfiguration of Jesus</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Luke" title="Gospel of Luke">Gospel of Luke</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herod_Antipas" title="Herod Antipas">Herod Antipas</a> hears some of the stories surrounding <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus Christ</a>. Some tell Herod that Jesus is John the Baptist (whom Herod had executed) come back to life. Others tell him that Jesus is Elijah.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Later in the same gospel, Jesus asks his disciples who the people say that he is. The apostles' answer includes Elijah among others.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>However Jesus' ministry had little in common with that of Elijah; in particular, he preached the forgiveness of one's enemies, while Elijah killed his. <a href="/wiki/Miracle" title="Miracle">Miracle</a> stories similar to those of Elijah were associated with Jesus (e. g. raising of the dead,<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> miraculous feeding).<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Jesus implicitly separates himself from Elijah when he rebukes James and John for desiring to call down fire upon an unwelcoming Samaritan village in a similar manner to Elijah.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Likewise, Jesus rebukes a potential follower who wanted first to return home to say farewell to his family, whereas Elijah permitted this of his replacement Elisha.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>During <a href="/wiki/Crucifixion_of_Jesus" title="Crucifixion of Jesus">Jesus' crucifixion</a>, some of the onlookers wonder if Elijah will come to rescue him,<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> as by the time of Jesus, Elijah had entered folklore as a rescuer of Jews in distress.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Transfiguration">Transfiguration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Transfiguration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Elijah makes an appearance in the New Testament during an incident known as the <a href="/wiki/Transfiguration_of_Jesus" title="Transfiguration of Jesus">Transfiguration</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the summit of an unnamed mount, Jesus' face begins to shine. The disciples who are with Him hear the voice of God announce that Jesus is "My beloved Son." The disciples also see Moses and Elijah appear and talk with Jesus. Peter is so struck by the experience that he asks Jesus if they should build three "tabernacles": one for Elijah, one for Jesus and one for Moses.</p>
<p>There is agreement among some Christian theologians that Elijah appears to hand over the responsibility of the prophets to Jesus as the woman by the well said to Jesus (John 4:19) "I perceive thou art a prophet." and Moses also likewise came to hand over the responsibility of the law for the divinely announced <a href="/wiki/Son_of_God" title="Son of God">Son of God</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_references">Other references</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Other references">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Elijah is mentioned four more times in the New Testament: in Luke, Romans, Hebrews, and James. In <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Luke#4:24" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/Luke">Luke 4:24–27</a>, Jesus uses Elijah as an example of rejected prophets. Jesus says, "No prophet is accepted in his own country," and then mentions Elijah, saying that there were many widows in Israel, but Elijah was sent to one in Phoenicia. In <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Romans#11:1" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/Romans">Romans 11:1–6</a>, Paul cites Elijah as an example of God's never forsaking his people (the Israelites). <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Hebrews#11:35" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/Hebrews">Hebrews 11:35</a> ("Women received their dead raised to life again...") refers to both Elijah raising the son of the widow of Zarephath and Elisha <a href="/wiki/Raising_of_the_son_of_the_woman_of_Shunem" title="Raising of the son of the woman of Shunem">raising the son of the woman of Shunem</a>, citing both Elijah and Elisha as Old Testament examples of faith.<sup id="cite_ref-HebrewsGill_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HebrewsGill-78">[78]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HebrewsHenry_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HebrewsHenry-79">[79]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HebrewsJamieson_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HebrewsJamieson-80">[80]</a></sup> In <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/James#5:16" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/James">James 5:16–18</a>, James says, "The effectual fervent <a href="/wiki/Prayer" title="Prayer">prayer</a> of a righteous man availeth much," and then cites Elijah's prayers which started and ended the famine in Israel as examples.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecclesiasticus">Ecclesiasticus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Ecclesiasticus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="toccolours" style="float: right; margin-left: 1em; margin-right: 2em; font-size: 85%; background:#c6dbf7; color:black; width:30em; max-width: 40%;" cellspacing="5">
<tr>
<td style="text-align: left;">
<p>"At the appointed time, it is written, you are destined<br />
to calm the wrath of God before it breaks out in fury,<br />
to turn the hearts of parents to their children,<br />
and to restore the tribes of Jacob."</p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align: left;">— A line in <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiasticus" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecclesiasticus">Ecclesiasticus</a> describing Elijah's mission (Ecclesiasticus 48:10).</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Ben_Sira" title="Ben Sira">Wisdom of Jesus ben Sira</a> (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://bible.oremus.org/?passage=Ecclesiasticus+48:10–48:10&amp;version=nrsv">Sirach 48:10</a>) his tasks are altered to: 1) herald the eschaton, 2) calm God’s fury, 3) restore familial peace, and 4) restore the 12 tribes.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prophet_saint">Prophet saint</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Prophet saint">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
