<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ElijahByLouisHersent.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/ElijahByLouisHersent.JPG/220px-ElijahByLouisHersent.JPG" width="220" height="262" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/ElijahByLouisHersent.JPG/330px-ElijahByLouisHersent.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c8/ElijahByLouisHersent.JPG 2x" data-file-width="425" data-file-height="506" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ElijahByLouisHersent.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Elijah reviving the Son of the Widow of <a href="/wiki/Sarepta" title="Sarepta">Zarephath</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Louis_Hersent" title="Louis Hersent">Louis Hersent</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Western_Christianity" title="Western Christianity">Western Christianity</a>, the Prophet Elijah is commemorated as a <a href="/wiki/Saint" title="Saint">saint</a> with a feast day on 20 July by the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Lutheran_Church%E2%80%93Missouri_Synod" title="Lutheran Church–Missouri Synod">Lutheran Church–Missouri Synod</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Catholics believe that he was unmarried and celibate.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Church</a> and those <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Catholic_Churches" title="Eastern Catholic Churches">Eastern Catholic Churches</a> which follow the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Rite" title="Byzantine Rite">Byzantine Rite</a>, he is commemorated on the same date (in the 21st century, <a href="/wiki/Julian_Calendar" class="mw-redirect" title="Julian Calendar">Julian Calendar</a> 20 July corresponds to <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_Calendar" class="mw-redirect" title="Gregorian Calendar">Gregorian Calendar</a> 2 August). He is greatly revered among the Orthodox as a model of the <a href="/wiki/Monasticism" title="Monasticism">contemplative life</a>. He is also commemorated on the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_liturgical_calendar" title="Eastern Orthodox liturgical calendar">Orthodox liturgical calendar</a> on the Sunday of the Holy Fathers (the Sunday before the <a href="/wiki/Christmas" title="Christmas">Nativity of the Lord</a>).</p>
<p>Elijah has been venerated as the <a href="/wiki/Patron_saint" title="Patron saint">patron saint</a> of Bosnia and Herzegovina since 26 August 1752, replacing <a href="/wiki/George_of_Lydda" class="mw-redirect" title="George of Lydda">George of Lydda</a> at the request of Bishop <a href="/wiki/Pavao_Dragi%C4%8Devi%C4%87" title="Pavao Dragičević">Pavao Dragičević</a>. The reasons for the replacement are unclear. It has been suggested that Elijah was chosen because of his importance to all three main religious groups in Bosnia and Herzegovina—<a href="/wiki/Catholicism_in_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholicism in Bosnia and Herzegovina">Catholics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Islam in Bosnia and Herzegovina">Muslims</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodoxy_in_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Eastern Orthodoxy in Bosnia and Herzegovina">Orthodox Christians</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Skoko_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Skoko-84">[84]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pope_Benedict_XIV" title="Pope Benedict XIV">Pope Benedict XIV</a> is said to have approved Bishop Dragičević's request with the remark that a wild nation deserved a wild patron.<sup id="cite_ref-Marti.C4.87_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marti.C4.87-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Carmelite_tradition">Carmelite tradition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Carmelite tradition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Elijah is revered as the spiritual Father and traditional founder of the Catholic religious Order of <a href="/wiki/Carmelites" title="Carmelites">Carmelites</a>. In addition to taking their name from Mt. Carmel where the first <a href="/wiki/Hermit" title="Hermit">hermits</a> of the order established themselves, the Calced Carmelite and <a href="/wiki/Discalced_Carmelites" title="Discalced Carmelites">Discalced Carmelite</a> traditions pertaining to Elijah focus upon the prophet’s withdrawal from public life.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> The medieval Carmelite <a href="/wiki/Book_of_the_First_Monks" title="Book of the First Monks">Book of the First Monks</a> offers some insight into the heart of the Orders' contemplative vocation and reverence for the prophet.</p>
<p>In the 17th Century the <a href="/wiki/Bollandist_Society" class="mw-redirect" title="Bollandist Society">Bollandist Society</a>, whose declared aim was to search out and classify materials concerning the saints venerated by the Church, and to print what seemed to be the most reliable sources of information <sup id="cite_ref-Thurston_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thurston-88">[88]</a></sup> entered into controversy with the Carmelites on this point. In writing of St. <a href="/wiki/Albert_Avogadro" class="mw-redirect" title="Albert Avogadro">Albert</a>, Patriarch of Jerusalem and author of the Carmelite rule, the Bollandist <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Papebroch" title="Daniel Papebroch">Daniel Papebroch</a> stated that the attribution of Carmelite origin to Elijah was insufficiently grounded. The Carmelites reacted strongly. From 1681 to 1698 a series of letters, pamphlets and other documents was issued by each side. The Carmelites were supported by a Spanish tribunal, while the Bollandists had the support of <a href="/wiki/Jean_de_Launoy" title="Jean de Launoy">Jean de Launoy</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sorbonne" title="Sorbonne">Sorbonne</a>. In November 1698, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Innocent_XII" title="Pope Innocent XII">Pope Innocent XII</a> ordered an end to the controversy.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Liturgical_commemorations">Liturgical commemorations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Liturgical commemorations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Prophet-Elias-Grk-ikon.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Prophet-Elias-Grk-ikon.png/220px-Prophet-Elias-Grk-ikon.png" width="220" height="351" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Prophet-Elias-Grk-ikon.png/330px-Prophet-Elias-Grk-ikon.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d9/Prophet-Elias-Grk-ikon.png 2x" data-file-width="350" data-file-height="558" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Prophet-Elias-Grk-ikon.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Elias on <a href="/wiki/Mount_Horeb" title="Mount Horeb">Mount Horeb</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Church" title="Greek Orthodox Church">Greek Orthodox</a> <a href="/wiki/Icon" title="Icon">icon</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Since most <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Churches" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Churches">Eastern Churches</a> either use Greek as their liturgical language or translated their liturgies from the Greek, <i>Elias</i> (or its modern <a href="/wiki/Iotacism" title="Iotacism">iotacized</a> form <i>Ilias</i>) is the form of the prophet's name used among most members of the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Church</a> and those <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Catholic_Churches" title="Eastern Catholic Churches">Eastern Catholic Churches</a> which follow the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Rite" title="Byzantine Rite">Byzantine Rite</a>.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Feast_day" class="mw-redirect" title="Feast day">feast day</a> of <a href="/wiki/Saint" title="Saint">saint</a> Elias falls on July 20 of the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church_liturgical_calendar" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Orthodox Church liturgical calendar">Orthodox liturgical calendar</a> (for those churches which follow the traditional <a href="/wiki/Julian_Calendar" class="mw-redirect" title="Julian Calendar">Julian Calendar</a>, July 20 currently falls on August 2 of the modern <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_Calendar" class="mw-redirect" title="Gregorian Calendar">Gregorian Calendar</a>). This day is a major holiday in Lebanon and is one of a handful of holidays there whose celebration is accompanied by a launching of fireworks by the general public. The full name of St. Elias in Lebanon translates to St. Elias the Living because it is believed that he did not die but rode his fiery chariot to heaven. The reference to the fiery chariot is likely why the Lebanese celebrate this holiday with fireworks.</p>
<p>Elias is also commemorated, together with all of the righteous persons of the Old Testament, on the Sunday of the Holy Fathers (the Sunday before the <a href="/wiki/Christmas" title="Christmas">Nativity of the Lord</a>).</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Apolytikion" title="Apolytikion">Apolytikion</a> in the Fourth Tone for St. Elias:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>The incarnate Angel, the Cornerstone of the Prophets, the second Forerunner of the Coming of Christ, the glorious Elias, who from above, sent down to Elisha the grace to dispel sickness and cleanse lepers, abounds therefore in healing for those who honor him.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Kontakion" title="Kontakion">Kontakion</a> in the Second Tone for St. Elias:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>O Prophet and foreseer of the great works of God, O greatly renowned Elias, who by your word held back the clouds of rain, intercede for us to the only Loving One.</p>
</blockquote>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pagan_associations_and_mountaintops"><span id="Helios"></span>Pagan associations and mountaintops</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Pagan associations and mountaintops">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Peryn#The_cult_of_Peroun_in_Peryn_and_in_Novgorod" title="Peryn">Peryn §&#160;The cult of Peroun in Peryn and in Novgorod</a></div>
<p>Starting in the fifth century, Elias is often connected with <a href="/wiki/Helios" title="Helios">Helios</a>, the Sun. The two words have very similar pronunciations in post-classical Greek; Elijah rode in his chariot of fire to heaven (<a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/2_Kings#2:11" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/2 Kings">2 Kings 2:11</a>) just as Helios drove the chariot of the sun across the sky; and the <a href="/wiki/Holocaust_(sacrifice)" title="Holocaust (sacrifice)">holocaust sacrifice</a> offered by Elijah and burned by fire from heaven (<a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/1_Kings#18:38" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/1 Kings">1 Kings 18:38</a>) corresponds to the sun warming the earth.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Coelius_Sedulius" title="Coelius Sedulius">Sedulius</a> writes poetically in the fifth century that the "bright path to glittering heaven" suits Elias both "in merits and name", as changing one letter makes his name "Helios"; but he does not identify the two.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> A homily entitled <i>De ascensione Heliae</i>, misattributed to <a href="/wiki/Chrysostom" class="mw-redirect" title="Chrysostom">Chrysostom</a>, claims that poets and painters use the ascension of Elijah as a model for their depictions of the sun, and says that "Elijah is really Helios". <a href="/wiki/Saint_Patrick" title="Saint Patrick">Saint Patrick</a> appears to conflate Helios and Elias.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> In modern times, much Greek folklore also connects Elias with the sun.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Greece, chapels and monasteries dedicated to Prophet Elias (Προφήτης Ηλίας) are often found on mountaintops, which themselves are often named after him. Since Wachsmuth (1864),<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> the usual explanation for this has been that Elias was identified with Helios, who had mountaintop shrines. But few shrines of Helios were on mountaintops, and sun-worship was subsumed by Apollo-worship by Christian times, and so could not be confused with Elias.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> The modern folklore is not good evidence for the <i>origin</i> of the association of the sun, Elias, and mountaintops.<sup id="cite_ref-cook_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cook-96">[96]</a></sup> Perhaps Elias is simply a "natural patron of high places".<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>The association of Elias with mountaintops seems to come from a different pagan tradition: Elias took on the attributes and the locales associated with <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a>, especially his associations with mountains and his powers over rain, thunder, lighting, and wind. When Elias prevailed over the priests of <a href="/wiki/Baal" title="Baal">Baal</a>, it was on <a href="/wiki/Mount_Carmel" title="Mount Carmel">Mount Carmel</a> (<a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/1_Kings#18:38" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/1 Kings">1 Kings 18:38</a>), which later became known as Mount St. Elias. When he spent forty days in a cave, it was on <a href="/wiki/Mount_Horeb" title="Mount Horeb">Mount Horeb</a> (<a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/1_Kings#19:8" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/1 Kings">1 Kings 19:8</a>). When Elias confronted <a href="/wiki/Ahab" title="Ahab">Ahab</a>, he stopped the rains for three years (<a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/1_Kings#17:1" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/1 Kings">1 Kings 17:1-18:1</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-cook_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cook-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>A map of mountain-cults of Zeus shows that most of these sites are now dedicated to Elias, including <a href="/wiki/Mount_Olympus" title="Mount Olympus">Mount Olympus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mount_Lykaion" title="Mount Lykaion">Mount Lykaion</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mount_Arachnaion&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Mount Arachnaion (page does not exist)">Mount Arachnaion</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mount_Taleton" class="mw-redirect" title="Mount Taleton">Mount Taleton</a> on the mainland, and <a href="/wiki/Mount_Kenaion" class="mw-redirect" title="Mount Kenaion">Mount Kenaion</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mount_Oche&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Mount Oche (page does not exist)">Mount Oche</a>, and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mount_Kynados&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Mount Kynados (page does not exist)">Mount Kynados</a> in the islands. Of these, the only one with a recorded tradition of a Helios cult is Mount Taleton.<sup id="cite_ref-cook_96-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cook-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>Elias is associated with pre-Christian lightning gods in many other European traditions.</p>
<p>Among Albanians, pilgrimages are made to mountaintops to ask for rain during the summer. One such tradition that is gaining popularity is the 2 August pilgrimage to <a href="/wiki/Ljuboten" title="Ljuboten">Ljuboten</a> on the Sharr mountains. Muslims refer to this day as <i>Aligjyn</i> ("Ali Day"), and it is believed that Ali becomes Elias at midday.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ilia_chariot.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Ilia_chariot.jpg/220px-Ilia_chariot.jpg" width="220" height="292" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Ilia_chariot.jpg/330px-Ilia_chariot.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Ilia_chariot.jpg/440px-Ilia_chariot.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1755" data-file-height="2333" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ilia_chariot.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
This common depiction of the prophet Elijah riding a flaming chariot across the sky resulted in <a href="/wiki/Syncretism" title="Syncretism">syncretistic</a> folklore among the <a href="/wiki/Slavs" title="Slavs">Slavs</a> incorporating pre-Christian motifs in the beliefs and rites regarding him in <a href="/wiki/Slavic_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Slavic culture">Slavic culture</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As Elijah was described as ascending into heaven in a fiery chariot, the Christian missionaries who converted <a href="/wiki/Slavic_tribes" class="mw-redirect" title="Slavic tribes">Slavic tribes</a> likely found him an ideal analogy for <a href="/wiki/Perun#Post-Christian_Perun" title="Perun">Perun</a>, the supreme <a href="/wiki/Slavic_mythology#Pantheon" class="mw-redirect" title="Slavic mythology">Slavic god</a> of storms, thunder and lightning bolts. In many <a href="/wiki/Slavic_countries" class="mw-redirect" title="Slavic countries">Slavic countries</a> Elijah is known as Elijah the Thunderer (Ilija Gromovnik), who drives the heavens in a chariot and administers rain and snow, thus actually taking the place of <a href="/wiki/Perun" title="Perun">Perun</a> in popular beliefs.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Perun is also sometimes conflated with the legendary hero <a href="/wiki/Ilya_Muromets" title="Ilya Muromets">Elijah of Murom</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mw_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mw-103">[103]</a></sup> The feast of St. Elias is known as <i>Ilinden</i> in <a href="/wiki/South_Slavic_languages" title="South Slavic languages">South Slavic</a>, and was chosen as the day of the <a href="/wiki/Ilinden-Preobrazhenie_Uprising" class="mw-redirect" title="Ilinden-Preobrazhenie Uprising">Ilinden-Preobrazhenie Uprising</a> in 1903; it is now the holiday of <a href="/wiki/Republic_Day_(Republic_of_Macedonia)" title="Republic Day (Republic of Macedonia)">Republic Day</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Macedonia" title="Republic of Macedonia">Republic of Macedonia</a>.</p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Estonian_folklore" title="Estonian folklore">Estonian folklore</a> Elijah is considered to be the successor of <a href="/wiki/Ukko" title="Ukko">Ukko</a>, the lightning spirit.<sup id="cite_ref-mw_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mw-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Georgian_mythology" title="Georgian mythology">Georgian mythology</a>, he replaces <a href="/w/index.php?title=Elwa&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Elwa (page does not exist)">Elwa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-mw_103-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mw-103">[103]</a></sup> A Georgian story about Elijah:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Once <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a>, the prophet Elijah, and St. George were going through <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>. When they became tired and hungry they stopped to dine. They saw a Georgian shepherd and decided to ask him to feed them. First, Elijah went up to the shepherd and asked him for a sheep. After the shepherd asked his identity Elijah said that, he was the one who sent him rain to get him a good profit from farming. The shepherd became angry at him and told him that he was the one who also sent thunderstorms, which destroyed the farms of poor widows. (After Elijah, Jesus and St. George attempt to get help and eventually succeed).<sup id="cite_ref-Gabidzashvili_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gabidzashvili-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Elias has other pagan associations: a modern legend about Elias mirrors precisely the legend of <a href="/wiki/Odysseus" title="Odysseus">Odysseus</a> seeking a place where the locals would not recognize an oar—hence the mountaintops.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Elijah_and_Elias_in_the_LDS_Church">Elijah and Elias in the LDS Church</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Elijah and Elias in the LDS Church">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/The_Church_of_Jesus_Christ_of_Latter-day_Saints" title="The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints">The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints</a> (LDS Church) acknowledges Elijah as a prophet. The LDS Church teaches that the Malachi prophecy of the return of Elijah was fulfilled on April 3, 1836, when Elijah visited the prophet and founder of the church, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Smith" title="Joseph Smith">Joseph Smith</a>, along with <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cowdery" title="Oliver Cowdery">Oliver Cowdery</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Kirtland_Temple" title="Kirtland Temple">Kirtland Temple</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Resurrection" title="Resurrection">resurrected</a> being.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> This event is chronicled in <span class="plainlinks"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://lds.org/scriptures/dc-testament/dc/110.13-16?lang=eng#12">Doctrine and Covenants 110:13-16</a></span>. This experience forms the basis for the church's focus on genealogy and family history and belief in the eternal nature of marriage and families.</p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Latter-day_Saint_theology" class="mw-redirect" title="Latter-day Saint theology">Latter-day Saint theology</a>, the name-title Elias is not always synonymous with Elijah and is often used for people other than the biblical prophet.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> According to Joseph Smith,</p>
<blockquote>
<p>The spirit of Elias is first, Elijah second, and Messiah last. Elias is a forerunner to prepare the way, and the spirit and power of Elijah is to come after, holding the keys of power, building the Temple to the capstone, placing the seals of the Melchizedek Priesthood upon the house of Israel, and making all things ready; then Messiah comes to His Temple, which is last of all.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>People to whom the title Elias is applied in Mormonism include <a href="/wiki/Noah" title="Noah">Noah</a>, the angel <a href="/wiki/Gabriel" title="Gabriel">Gabriel</a> (who is considered to be the same person as Noah in Mormon doctrine), Elijah, <a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">John the Baptist</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_the_Apostle" title="John the Apostle">John the Apostle</a>, and an unspecified man who was a contemporary of <a href="/wiki/Abraham" title="Abraham">Abraham</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-urlElias_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-urlElias-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>Detractors of Mormonism have often alleged that Smith, in whose time and place the King James Version was the only available English translation of the Bible, simply failed to grasp the fact that the Elijah of the Old Testament and the Elias of the New Testament are the same person.<sup id="cite_ref-burton74_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-burton74-110">[110]</a></sup> Latter-day Saints deny this and say that the difference they make between the two is deliberate and prophetic. The names Elias and Elijah refer to one who prepares the way for the coming of the Lord. This is applicable to John the Baptist coming to prepare the way for the Lord and His baptism; it also refers to Elijah appearing during the transfiguration to prepare for Jesus by restoring keys of sealing power.<sup id="cite_ref-burton74_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-burton74-110">[110]</a></sup> Jesus then gave this power to the Twelve saying, "Verily I say unto you, Whatsoever ye shall bind on earth shall be bound in heaven: and whatsoever ye shall loose on earth shall be loosed in heaven." <sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Elijah_in_Islam">Elijah in Islam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Elijah in Islam">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Khidr_and_elijah.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Khidr_and_elijah.jpg/220px-Khidr_and_elijah.jpg" width="220" height="340" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Khidr_and_elijah.jpg/330px-Khidr_and_elijah.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Khidr_and_elijah.jpg/440px-Khidr_and_elijah.jpg 2x" data-file-width="762" data-file-height="1178" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Khidr_and_elijah.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Khidr" title="Khidr">Khizr</a> and Elijah Praying in <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a></i>; <a href="/wiki/Persian_miniature" title="Persian miniature">Persian miniature</a> from an <a href="/wiki/Illuminated_manuscript" title="Illuminated manuscript">illuminated manuscript</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/Stories_of_the_Prophets" class="mw-redirect" title="Stories of the Prophets">Stories of the Prophets</a></i> (c. 427 <a href="/wiki/Hijri_year" title="Hijri year">AH</a>/ 1036 <a href="/wiki/AD" class="mw-redirect" title="AD">AD</a>)</div>
</div>
</div>

<td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;"><a href="/wiki/File:Allah-green.svg" class="image"><img alt="Allah-green.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Allah-green.svg/15px-Allah-green.svg.png" width="15" height="16" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Allah-green.svg/23px-Allah-green.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Allah-green.svg/31px-Allah-green.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="206" data-file-height="215" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Islam" title="Portal:Islam">Islam portal</a></td>

<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mir_Sayyid_Ali_Ilia_prorok.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Mir_Sayyid_Ali_Ilia_prorok.jpg/220px-Mir_Sayyid_Ali_Ilia_prorok.jpg" width="220" height="289" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Mir_Sayyid_Ali_Ilia_prorok.jpg/330px-Mir_Sayyid_Ali_Ilia_prorok.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Mir_Sayyid_Ali_Ilia_prorok.jpg/440px-Mir_Sayyid_Ali_Ilia_prorok.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1823" data-file-height="2396" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mir_Sayyid_Ali_Ilia_prorok.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Prophet Elijah Rescuing Nur ad-Dahr from the Sea</i>, a scene from the <i><a href="/wiki/Hamzanama" title="Hamzanama">Hamzanama</a></i>, here imagined in a <a href="/wiki/Persian_miniature" title="Persian miniature">Persian miniature</a> by Mir Sayyid Ali (c. 1550 AD)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Elijah</b> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>: <b>إلياس</b> or <b>إليا</b>; <i>Ilyas</i> or <i>Ilya</i>) is also mentioned as a <a href="/wiki/Prophets_of_Islam" class="mw-redirect" title="Prophets of Islam">prophet</a> in the Qur'an, <a href="/wiki/Al-An%27am" title="Al-An'am">al-An'am</a> 85. Elijah's narrative in the <a href="/wiki/Qur%27an" class="mw-redirect" title="Qur'an">Qur'an</a> and later <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> <a href="/wiki/Tradition" title="Tradition">tradition</a> resembles closely that in the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">Hebrew Bible</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> <a href="/wiki/Literature" title="Literature">literature</a> records Elijah's primary prophesying as taking place during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Ahab" title="Ahab">Ahab</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jezebel" title="Jezebel">Jezebel</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Ahaziah_of_Israel" title="Ahaziah of Israel">Ahaziah</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> He is seen by <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslims</a> to be the prophetic predecessor to <a href="/wiki/Elisha" title="Elisha">Elisha</a>. While neither the Bible nor the Qur'an mentions the genealogy of Elijah, some scholars of Islam believe he may have come from the priestly family of the prophet <a href="/wiki/Aaron#Islam" title="Aaron">Aaron</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> Elijah is rarely associated with <a href="/wiki/Islamic_eschatology" title="Islamic eschatology">Islamic eschatology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> views <a href="/wiki/Jesus_in_Islam" title="Jesus in Islam">Jesus</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Messiah" title="Messiah">Messiah</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> However, Elijah is expected to come back along with the mysterious figure known as <a href="/wiki/Khidr" title="Khidr">Khidr</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Last_Judgment" title="Last Judgment">Last Judgment</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> Elijah's figure has been identified with a number of other <a href="/wiki/Prophet" title="Prophet">prophets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saint" title="Saint">saints</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Idris_(prophet)" title="Idris (prophet)">Idris</a>, which is believed by some <a href="/wiki/Scholar" class="mw-redirect" title="Scholar">scholars</a> to have been another name for Elijah,<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> and Khidr.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Islamic" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic">Islamic</a> <a href="/wiki/Legend" title="Legend">legend</a> later developed the figure of Elijah, greatly embellishing upon his attributes, and some apocryphal literature gave Elijah the status of a half-human, half-angel.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> Elijah also appears in later works of literature, including the <i><a href="/wiki/Hamzanama" title="Hamzanama">Hamzanama</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Quran">Quran</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Quran">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Elijah is mentioned in the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a>, where his preaching is recounted in a concise manner. The Quran narrates that Elijah told his people to come to the worship of God and to leave the worship of <a href="/wiki/Baal" title="Baal">Baal</a>, the primary <a href="/wiki/Idolatry" title="Idolatry">idol</a> of the area. The Quran states, "Verily Elijah was one of the apostles. When he said to his people: "Will you not fear <a href="/wiki/Allah" title="Allah">God</a>? "Will ye call upon Ba'al and leave the Best of Creators, God, your L<span class="smallcaps" style="font-size:83%">ORD</span> and Cherisher and the L<span class="smallcaps" style="font-size:83%">ORD</span> and Cherisher of your fathers of old?" <a href="/wiki/As-Saaffat" title="As-Saaffat">As-Saaffat</a> 123–126<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Quran makes it clear that the majority of Elijah's people denied the prophet and continued to follow idolatry. However, it mentions that a small number of devoted servants of <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">God</a> among them followed Elijah and believed in and worshiped God. The Quran states, "They denied him (Elijah), and will surely be brought to punishment, Except the sincere and devoted Servants of God (among them). And We left his (memory) for posterity." <a href="/wiki/As-Saaffat" title="As-Saaffat">As-Saaffat</a> 127–128<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the Quran, God praises Elijah in two places:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Peace be upon Elijah! This is how We reward those who do good. He is truly among our believing servants.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Quran, chapter 37 (<a href="/wiki/As-Saaffat" title="As-Saaffat">As-Saaffat</a>), <a href="/wiki/Ayah" title="Ayah">verse</a> 129–132<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
</li>
</ul>
<ul>
<li>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>And Zachariah and John and Jesus and Elijah, they were all from among the righteous</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Quran, chapter 6 (<a href="/wiki/Al-An%27am" title="Al-An'am">Al-An'am</a>), verse 85<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Numerous commentators, including <a href="/wiki/Abdullah_Yusuf_Ali" title="Abdullah Yusuf Ali">Abdullah Yusuf Ali</a>, have offered commentary on VI: 85 saying that Elijah, <a href="/wiki/Zechariah_(priest)" title="Zechariah (priest)">Zechariah</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">John the Baptist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a> were all spiritually connected. <a href="/wiki/Abdullah_Yusuf_Ali" title="Abdullah Yusuf Ali">Abdullah Yusuf Ali</a> says, "The third group consists not of men of action, but Preachers of Truth, who led solitary lives. Their epithet is: "the Righteous." They form a connected group round Jesus. Zachariah was the father of John the Baptist, who is referenced as "Elias, which was for to come" (Matt 11:14); and Elias is said to have been present and talked to Jesus at the Transfiguration on the Mount (Matt. 17:3)."<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature_and_tradition">Literature and tradition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Literature and tradition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Muslim <a href="/wiki/Literature" title="Literature">literature</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tradition" title="Tradition">tradition</a> recounts that Elijah preached to the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Israel_(Samaria)" title="Kingdom of Israel (Samaria)">Kingdom of Israel</a>, ruled over by <a href="/wiki/Ahab" title="Ahab">Ahab</a> and later his son <a href="/wiki/Ahaziah_of_Israel" title="Ahaziah of Israel">Ahaziah</a>. He is believed to have been a "prophet of the desert—like <a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">John the Baptist</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> Elijah is believed to have preached with zeal to Ahab and his wife <a href="/wiki/Jezebel" title="Jezebel">Jezebel</a>, who according to Muslim tradition was partly responsible for the worship of <a href="/wiki/False_god" title="False god">false idols</a> in this area. Muslims believe that it was because the majority of people refused to listen to Elijah that Elisha had to continue preaching the message of <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">God</a> to Israel after him.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>Elijah has been the subject of legends and folktales in Muslim culture, usually involving his meeting with Khidr, and in one legend, with <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> himself.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> Most such legends, however, are regarded as folktales rather than actual events. In <a href="/wiki/Islamic" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic">Islamic</a> <a href="/wiki/Mysticism" title="Mysticism">mysticism</a>, however, Elijah is associated closely with the sage <a href="/wiki/Khidr" title="Khidr">Khidr</a>. One legend reported that Elijah and <a href="/wiki/Khidr" title="Khidr">Khidr</a> met together every year in <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a> to go on the <a href="/wiki/Pilgrimage" title="Pilgrimage">pilgrimage</a> to <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> Elijah appears also in the <i><a href="/wiki/Hamzanama" title="Hamzanama">Hamzanama</a></i> numerous times, where he is spoken of as being the brother of <a href="/wiki/Khidr" title="Khidr">Khidr</a> as well as one who drunk from the <a href="/wiki/Fountain_of_Youth" title="Fountain of Youth">Fountain of Youth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although most <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> scholars believed that Elijah preached in <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, some early commentators on the <a href="/wiki/Qur%27an" class="mw-redirect" title="Qur'an">Qur'an</a> stated that Elijah was sent to <a href="/wiki/Baalbek" title="Baalbek">Baalbek</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> Modern scholars have rejected this claim, stating that the connection of the city with Elijah would have been made because of the first half of the city's name, that of <i><a href="/wiki/Baal" title="Baal">Baal</a></i>, which was the deity that Elijah exhorted his people to stop worshiping. Scholars who reject identification of Elijah's town with Baalbek further argue that the town of Baalbek is not mentioned with the narrative of Elijah in either the Qur'an or the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">Hebrew Bible</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span id="Elijah_in_the_Bahá'i_Faith"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Elijah_in_the_Bah.C3.A1.27i_Faith">Elijah in the Bahá'i Faith</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Elijah in the Bahá'i Faith">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%27%C3%AD_Faith" title="Bahá'í Faith">Bahá'í Faith</a>, the <a href="/wiki/B%C3%A1b" title="Báb">Báb</a>, founder of the <a href="/wiki/B%C3%A1b%C3%AD" class="mw-redirect" title="Bábí">Bábí</a> Faith, is believed to be the return of Elijah and <a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">John the Baptist</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-gpb_58_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gpb_58-132">[132]</a></sup> Both Elijah and John the Baptist are considered to be Lesser <a href="/wiki/Prophet" title="Prophet">Prophets</a>, whose stations are below that of a <a href="/wiki/Manifestation_of_God" title="Manifestation of God">Manifestation of God</a> like Jesus Christ, Buddha, Muhammad, the Báb or <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%27u%27ll%C3%A1h" title="Bahá'u'lláh">Bahá'u'lláh</a>. The Báb is buried on Mount Carmel, where Elijah had his confrontation with the prophets of Baal.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversies">Controversies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Controversies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Miracle_of_the_ravens">Miracle of the ravens</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Miracle of the ravens">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lanfranco_Elie_nourri_par_le_corbeau.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Lanfranco_Elie_nourri_par_le_corbeau.jpg/220px-Lanfranco_Elie_nourri_par_le_corbeau.jpg" width="220" height="241" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Lanfranco_Elie_nourri_par_le_corbeau.jpg/330px-Lanfranco_Elie_nourri_par_le_corbeau.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Lanfranco_Elie_nourri_par_le_corbeau.jpg/440px-Lanfranco_Elie_nourri_par_le_corbeau.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1676" data-file-height="1834" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lanfranco_Elie_nourri_par_le_corbeau.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Elijah fed by the ravens</i>, by <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Lanfranco" title="Giovanni Lanfranco">Giovanni Lanfranco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_des_beaux-arts_de_Marseille" title="Musée des beaux-arts de Marseille">Musée des beaux-arts de Marseille</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>That <i>ravens</i> fed Elijah by the brook <a href="/wiki/Chorath" title="Chorath">Chorath</a> has been questioned. The Hebrew text at <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt09a17.htm#4">1 Kings 17:4–6</a> uses the word <span lang="he" xml:lang="he">עֹרְבִים</span> <i>`ōrvīm</i>, which means <i>ravens</i>, but with a different vocalization might equally mean <i>Arabs</i>. The <a href="/wiki/Septuagint" title="Septuagint">Septuagint</a> has <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">κορακες</span>, <i>ravens</i>, and other traditional translations followed.</p>
<p>Alternatives have been proposed for many years; for example <a href="/wiki/Adam_Clarke" title="Adam Clarke">Adam Clarke</a> treats it as a discussion already of long standing.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> Objections to the traditional translation are that ravens are ritually unclean (see <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0311.htm#13">Leviticus 11:13–17</a>) as well as physically dirty; it is difficult to imagine any method of delivery of the food which is not disgusting. The parallelism with the incident that follows, where Elijah is fed by the widow, also suggests a human, if mildly improbable, agent.</p>
<p>Prof. John Gray chooses <i>Arabs</i>, saying "We adopt this reading solely because of its congruity with the sequel, where Elijah is fed by an alien Phoenician woman."<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> His translation of the verses in question is:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>And the word of Jehovah came to Elijah saying, Go hence and turn eastward and hide thyself in the Wadi Chorath east of the Jordan, and it shall be that thou shalt drink of the wadi, and I have commanded the Arabs to feed thee there. And he went and did according to the word of Jehovah and went and dwelt in the Wadi Chorath east of the Jordan. And the Arabs brought him bread in the morning and flesh in the evening and he would drink of the wadi.</p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ascension_into_the_heavens">Ascension into the heavens</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Ascension into the heavens">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_John" title="Gospel of John">Gospel of John</a>, Jesus says: "And no man hath ascended up to heaven, but he that came down from heaven, <i>[even]</i> the Son of man which is in heaven." (John 3:13)</p>
<p>Traditionally Christianity interprets the <a href="/wiki/Names_and_titles_of_Jesus_in_the_New_Testament#Son_of_Man" title="Names and titles of Jesus in the New Testament">"Son of Man"</a> as a title of Jesus, but this has never been an article of faith and there are other interpretations. Further interpreting this quote, some Christians believe that Elijah was not assumed into heaven but simply transferred to another assignment either in heaven<sup id="cite_ref-djytel_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-djytel-136">[136]</a></sup> or with <a href="/wiki/Jehoram_of_Judah" title="Jehoram of Judah">King Jehoram of Judah</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-djytel_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-djytel-136">[136]</a></sup> The prophets reacted in such a way that makes sense if he was carried away, and not simply straight up (<a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/2_Kings#2:16" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/2 Kings">2 Kings 2:16</a>).</p>
<p>The question of whether Elijah was in heaven or elsewhere on earth depends partly on the view of the letter Jehoram received from Elijah in 2 Chronicles 21 after Elijah had ascended. Some have suggested that the letter was written before Elijah ascended, but only delivered later.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> The rabbinical <a href="/wiki/Seder_Olam_Rabbah" title="Seder Olam Rabbah">Seder Olam</a> explains that the letter was delivered seven years after his ascension.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> This is also a possible explanation for some variation in manuscripts of <a href="/wiki/Josephus" title="Josephus">Josephus</a>' <i><a href="/wiki/Antiquities_of_the_Jews" title="Antiquities of the Jews">Antiquities of the Jews</a></i> when dealing with this issue.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> Others have argued that Elijah was only "caught away" such as Philip in Acts 8:39<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Lightfoot" title="John Lightfoot">John Lightfoot</a> reasoned that it must have been a different Elijah.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p>Elijah's name typically occurs in <a href="/wiki/Entering_heaven_alive#Judaism" class="mw-redirect" title="Entering heaven alive">Jewish lists of those who have entered heaven alive</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Return">Return</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Return">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Centuries after his departure the Jewish nation awaits the coming of Elijah to precede the coming of the Messiah. For many Christians this prophecy was fulfilled in the gospels, where he appears during the Transfiguration alongside Moses (<a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Matthew#17:9" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/Matthew">Matthew 17:9–13</a>). Commentators have said that Moses' appearance represented the law, while Elijah's appearance represented the prophets.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints believes that Elijah returned on April 3, 1836 in an appearance to <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Smith" title="Joseph Smith">Joseph Smith</a> and Oliver Cowdery, fulfilling the prophecy in Malachi.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%27%C3%AD_Faith" title="Bahá'í Faith">Bahá'í Faith</a> believes Elijah returned as the <a href="/wiki/Biblical" class="mw-redirect" title="Biblical">biblical</a> <a href="/wiki/Prophet" title="Prophet">prophet</a> <a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">John the Baptist</a> and as the <a href="/wiki/B%C3%A1b" title="Báb">Báb</a> who founded the <a href="/wiki/B%C3%A1b%C3%AD" class="mw-redirect" title="Bábí">Bábí</a> Faith in 1844.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Nation_of_Islam" title="Nation of Islam">Nation of Islam</a> believes Elijah returned as <a href="/wiki/Elijah_Muhammad" title="Elijah Muhammad">Elijah Muhammad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Black_separatist" class="mw-redirect" title="Black separatist">black separatist</a> religious leader (who claimed to be a "messenger", not a prophet). This is considered less important than their belief that Allah himself showed up in the person of <a href="/wiki/Fard_Muhammad" class="mw-redirect" title="Fard Muhammad">Fard Muhammad</a>, the founder of the group. It differs notably from most beliefs about Elijah, in that his re-appearance is usually the precursor to a greater one's appearance, rather than an afterthought.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Elijah_in_arts_and_literature">Elijah in arts and literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Elijah in arts and literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li>Perhaps the best-known representation of the story of Elijah is <a href="/wiki/Felix_Mendelssohn" title="Felix Mendelssohn">Felix Mendelssohn</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Oratorio" title="Oratorio">oratorio</a> "<a href="/wiki/Elijah_(oratorio)" title="Elijah (oratorio)">Elijah</a>". The oratorio chronicles many episodes of Elijah's life, including his challenge to Ahab and the contest of the gods, the miracle of raising the dead, and his ascension into heaven. Composed and premiered in 1846, the oratorio was criticized by members of the <a href="/wiki/New_German_School" title="New German School">New German School</a> but nonetheless remains one of the most popular Romantic choral-orchestral works in the repertoire.</li>
<li>In <i><a href="/wiki/Orlando_Furioso" title="Orlando Furioso">Orlando Furioso</a></i>, the English knight <a href="/wiki/Astolfo" title="Astolfo">Astolfo</a> flies up to the moon in Elijah's flaming chariot.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Elijah_Rock" title="Elijah Rock">Elijah Rock</a> is a traditional <a href="/wiki/Christian" title="Christian">Christian</a> <a href="/wiki/Spiritual_(music)" title="Spiritual (music)">spiritual</a> about Elijah, also sometimes used by Jewish youth groups.</li>
<li>"Go Like Elijah" is a song by the American rock-pop-jazz songwriter <a href="/wiki/Chi_Coltrane" title="Chi Coltrane">Chi Coltrane</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lorenzetto" title="Lorenzetto">Lorenzetto</a> created a statue of Elijah with assistance of the young sculptor <a href="/wiki/Raffaello_da_Montelupo" title="Raffaello da Montelupo">Raffaello da Montelupo</a>, using designs by Raphael.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Fifth_Mountain" title="The Fifth Mountain">The Fifth Mountain</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Paulo_Coelho" title="Paulo Coelho">Paulo Coelho</a> is based on the story of Elijah.</li>
<li>Christian metal band <a href="/wiki/Disciple_(band)" title="Disciple (band)">Disciple</a> released the song "God of Elijah" on their 2001 album <a href="/wiki/By_God" title="By God">By God</a>. The theme of the song is the challenge Elijah placed against Ahab between <a href="/wiki/Baal" title="Baal">Baal</a> and the god of Israel.</li>
<li>The roots-fusion band <a href="/wiki/Seatrain_(band)" title="Seatrain (band)">Seatrain</a> records, on the albums of the same name (1970), bandmember Peter Rowans song Waiting for Elijah, alluding to Elijahs second coming, see Old Testament references above.</li>
<li>From 1974 to 1976 <a href="/wiki/Philip_K._Dick" title="Philip K. Dick">Philip K. Dick</a> believed himself to be <a href="/wiki/Spirit_possession" title="Spirit possession">possessed</a> by the spirit of Elijah.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> He later included Elijah (as Elias Tate) in his novel <i><a href="/wiki/The_Divine_Invasion" title="The Divine Invasion">The Divine Invasion</a></i>.</li>
<li>On <a href="/wiki/Ryan_Adams" title="Ryan Adams">Ryan Adams</a>' 2005 album <i><a href="/wiki/29_(album)" title="29 (album)">29</a></i>, the song "Voices" speaks of Elijah, alluding to Elijah being the prophet of destruction.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Journeys_With_Elijah:_Eight_Tales_of_the_Prophet" title="Journeys With Elijah: Eight Tales of the Prophet">Journeys With Elijah: Eight Tales of the Prophet</a></i>, book by Barbara Goldin and illustrated by <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Pinkney" title="Jerry Pinkney">Jerry Pinkney</a></li>
<li>In 1996, <a href="/wiki/Robin_Mark" title="Robin Mark">Robin Mark</a> created a praise song entitled <i>Days of Elijah</i>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cormac_McCarthy" title="Cormac McCarthy">Cormac McCarthy</a>'s post-apocalyptic novel <i><a href="/wiki/The_Road" title="The Road">The Road</a></i> (2006) features an old man who ambiguously refers to himself as Ely.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Elijah_Baley" title="Elijah Baley">Elijah</a> ("Lije") is the name of the protagonist in three novels of <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Asimov" title="Isaac Asimov">Isaac Asimov</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Robot_series_(Asimov)" title="Robot series (Asimov)"><i>Robot</i> series</a>. He is familiar with biblical stories and sometimes relates them in the narrative or in discussion with his <a href="/wiki/R._Daneel_Olivaw" title="R. Daneel Olivaw">robot partner</a> who was built on a world devoid of religion. His wife is ironically named Jezebel.</li>
<li>The popular movie <i><a href="/wiki/Chariots_of_Fire" title="Chariots of Fire">Chariots of Fire</a></i> alludes to the William Blake poem <a href="/wiki/And_did_those_feet_in_ancient_time" title="And did those feet in ancient time">And did those feet in ancient time</a>, which in turn alludes to the Elijah story.</li>
<li>Elijah was played by <a href="/wiki/John_Hoyt" title="John Hoyt">John Hoyt</a> in the 1953 film <i><a href="/wiki/Sins_of_Jezebel" title="Sins of Jezebel">Sins of Jezebel</a></i>.</li>
<li>A series of paintings by <a href="/wiki/Clive_Hicks-Jenkins" title="Clive Hicks-Jenkins">Clive Hicks-Jenkins</a> around 2003–07 depicted Elijah being fed by a raven, inspired by fragments of a Tuscan altarpiece in <a href="/wiki/Christ_Church_Picture_Gallery" title="Christ Church Picture Gallery">Christ Church Picture Gallery</a> in Oxford.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup></li>
<li>Referenced in the song "It Was Written", by <a href="/wiki/Damian_Marley" title="Damian Marley">Damian Marley</a>, featuring <a href="/wiki/Capleton" title="Capleton">Capleton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Drag-On" title="Drag-On">Drag-On</a>.</li>
<li>Referenced in the movie <i><a href="/wiki/The_Book_of_Eli" title="The Book of Eli">The Book of Eli</a></i>, starring <a href="/wiki/Denzel_Washington" title="Denzel Washington">Denzel Washington</a> in the title role as the man on a mission in a post-apocalyptic world to deliver the Bible for safe-keeping.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/I._L._Peretz" title="I. L. Peretz">I. L. Peretz</a> wrote <i>The Magician</i>, which was illustrated by <a href="/wiki/Marc_Chagall" title="Marc Chagall">Marc Chagall</a> in 1917, about Elijah.<sup id="cite_ref-WDL_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WDL-149">[149]</a></sup></li>
<li>Early in <i><a href="/wiki/Moby-Dick" title="Moby-Dick">Moby-Dick</a></i>, Ishmael and Queequeg run into a scarred and deformed man named Elijah, a prophet (or perhaps merely a frightening stranger) who hints to them the perils of signing aboard Ahab's ship, the <i>Pequod</i>.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elijah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li id="cite_note-burton74-110"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-burton74_110-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-burton74_110-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFBurton1974" class="citation"><a href="/wiki/Theodore_M._Burton" title="Theodore M. Burton">Burton, Theodore M. Burton</a> (May 1974), <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://lds.org/ensign/1974/05/the-power-of-elijah?lang=eng">"The Power of Elijah"</a>, <i>Ensign</i></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AElijah&amp;rft.atitle=The+Power+of+Elijah&amp;rft.aufirst=Theodore+M.+Burton&amp;rft.aulast=Burton&amp;rft.date=1974-05&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Ensign&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Flds.org%2Fensign%2F1974%2F05%2Fthe-power-of-elijah%3Flang%3Deng&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-111"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-111">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Matthew#18:18" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/Matthew">Matthew 18:18</a></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-112"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-112">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Abdullah_Yusuf_Ali" title="Abdullah Yusuf Ali">Abdullah Yusuf Ali</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Holy_Qur%27an:_Text,_Translation_and_Commentary" title="The Holy Qur'an: Text, Translation and Commentary">The Holy Qur'an: Text, Translation and Commentary</a></i>, Note. <b>4112</b>: "Elias is the same as Elijah, whose story will be found in the Old Testament in I Kings 17–19. and 2 Kings 1–2. Elijah lived in the reign of Ahab (896–874 BC) and Ahaziah (874–872 BC), kings of the (northern) kingdom of Israel or Samaria. He was a prophet of the desert, like John the Baptist, unlike our holy Prophet, who took part in, controlled, and guided all the affairs of his people. Both Ahab and Azariah were prone to lapse into the worship of Baal, the sun-god worshipped in Syria. That worship also included the worship of nature-powers and procreative powers, as in the Hindu worship of the Lingam, and led to many abuses. King Ahab had married a princess of Sidon, Jezebel, a wicked woman who led her husband to forsake Allah and adopt Baal-worship."Elijah denounced all Ahab's sins as well as the sins of Ahaziah and had to flee for his life. Eventually, according to the Old Testament (4 Kings, 2:11) he was taken up in a whirlwind to heaven in a chariot of fire after he had left his mantle with Elisha the prophet."</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-139"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-139">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Begg C. <i>Josephus' story of the later monarchy: (AJ 9, 1–10, 185)</i> Section "Elijah's Letter" p119</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-140"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-140">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Ron Abel <i>Wrested Scriptures</i> "There is evidence that Elijah was back on earth after he was taken away in the whirlwind. It can be shown that a letter was received by Jehoram, King of Judah, from Elijah, after Elijah was taken to heaven. Either the letter was written before he went to heaven and delivered by a messenger on earth (unlikely), or Elijah was "caught away" as was Philip from the Gaza Road to Azotas, (about 17 miles, Acts 8:39, 40) for an unspecified purpose and returned to the earth. Consider the evidence: 1. Elijah had been taken to heaven in a whirlwind. (2 Kings 2:11). 2. Elisha had taken over the duties of Elijah in the reign of Jehoshaphat. (2 Kings 3:10, 11)., 3. Jehoram received a letter from Elijah, the prophet. (2 Chron. 21:1, 9–12). King Jehoram reigned after Jehoshaphat. (2 Chron. 21:1)".</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-141"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-141">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Barrett Richard A.F. <i>A synopsis of criticisms upon those passages</i>, Volume 3, Part 1 p. 234 1847 "But our Dr. Lightfoot is of opinion, that it is not meant of that Elijah, who was carried up to heaven, but of another of his name, who sent this letter"</span></li>

The <a href="/wiki/Prophets_in_Islam" class="mw-redirect" title="Prophets in Islam">Islamic prophets</a> above are only the ones mentioned by name in the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a>.</div>
